      I S K NOWLEDGE IN M ULTILINGUAL L ANGUAGE M OD -

008
009                                                                                 A BSTRACT
010
011            Few works study the variation and cross-lingual consistency of factual knowledge
012            embedded in multilingual models. However, cross-lingual consistency should be
013            considered to assess cross-lingual transferability, maintain the factuality of the
014            modelвҖҷs knowledge across languages, and preserve the parity of language model
015            performance. We are thus interested in analyzing, evaluating, and interpreting
016            cross-lingual consistency for factual knowledge. We apply interpretability ap-
017
               proaches to analyze a modelвҖҷs behavior in cross-lingual contexts, discovering that
               multilingual models show different levels of consistency, subject to either lan-
018
               guage families or linguistic factors. Further, we identify a cross-lingual consis-
019
               tency bottleneck manifested in middle layers. To mitigate this problem, we try vo-
020            cabulary expansion, additional cross-lingual objectives, adding biases from mono-
021            lingual inputs, multi-task fine-tuning, and code-switching training. We find that
022            all these methods, except for multi-task fine-tuning, boost cross-lingual consis-
023            tency to some extent, with cross-lingual supervision and code-switching training
024            offering the best improvement.
025
026   1   I NTRODUCTION
027
028                                                         FregeвҖҷs theory of reference states that the truth-value of a sentence is a function of the references

036
      the reference of a sub-sentential expression as the object singled out by the name.
037
038
039
      FregeвҖҷs theory of reference (Frege, 1892) indicates that the knowledge conveyed by a sentence de-
      pends on the references of the expressions that compose the sentence. A salient aspect of humanity
040
      is that while people may speak different languages, they can share common knowledge. Thus, ref-
041
      erences and knowledge should be consistent across languages, and a multilingual model serving as
042   a knowledge base (Gupta & Srikumar, 2021; Kassner et al., 2021; Hu et al., 2024) should provide
043   consistent knowledge when queried in different languages. Not only does this theory contribute to
044   the cross-lingual performance and maintain the knowledge across languages, but it ensures parity
045   and self-consistency of model performance (Hupkes et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2023). This motivates
046   us to evaluate the knowledge consistency of multilingual language models across languages.
047
      Although recent advances have shown that multilingual models are effective for cross-lingual trans-
048   fer and generalization (Conneau et al., 2020; Xue et al., 2020; Hu et al., 2020; Muennighoff et al.,
049   2023), Kassner et al. (2021); Fierro & SГёgaard (2022); Qi et al. (2023) reported that the prediction
050   varies from one language to others when recalling knowledge in different languages. For given par-
051   allel statements, a language model may output predictions for a particular query that differs from
052   one obtained from the queryвҖҷs translation. This examination implicitly instantiates FregeвҖҷs theory of
053   reference to check knowledge consistency across languages that parallel statements with the same
      references for sub-sentential expressions such as entities should have the same knowledge.


                                                                                                       1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      Inspired by that theory, we hypothesize that multilingual language models recall consistent factual
055   knowledge for coreferential statements in cross-lingual settings. To evaluate this hypothesis (Fig-
056   ure 1), we create code-mixed coreferential statements from monolingual statements by substituting
057   a subject entity with an equivalent one in another language that shares the same reference and at-
058   tempt to answer two questions: 1) do multilingual language models recall factual knowledge for the
059   coreferential statements in a similar manner on different languages? and 2) how does the mecha-
060   nism of multilingual language models work on the incorporation between entities or references in
061   cross-lingual settings? Our study is related to a broader linguistic phenomenon of entity-level code-
062   switching: an entity code-switches between two languages without changing the reference. More
063
      recently, we share a similar goal with knowledge incorporation and editing (Beniwal et al., 2024;
      Li et al., 2024), as we incorporate a coreferential entity from other languages for factual knowledge
064
      recall in cross-lingual settings. Our main findings are:
065
066              вҖў Multilingual language models can leverage coreferential entities across similar languages,
067                e.g., similar writing scripts, to maintain cross-lingual knowledge consistency when recall-
068                ing factual knowledge but worse across dissimilar languages.
069
                 вҖў Scaling models is not a promising strategy to improve cross-lingual consistency as we
070                observed a bottleneck starting from middle layers across different model families and sizes.
071
072
                 вҖў Consistency patterns in feed-forward neurons and subjectвҖ“object attention scores are indis-
                   tinguishable for similar languages and distinguishable for dissimilar languages.
073
074   Based on our main findings, we further evaluate several mitigations that could resolve inconsistency
075   issues with the results described below, particularly those observed in dissimilar languages.
076
077              вҖў There is a partial causality between adding monolingual biases and improving cross-lingual
078                knowledge consistency. Thus, adding bias could be a potential method to calibrate consis-
079                tency across languages while we do need to think a better way to incorporate such bias.
080              вҖў Expanding the multilingual vocabulary, adding word alignment objective, and code-
081                switching training can improve the cross-lingual consistency as such method helps in align-
082                ing coreferential entities across languages to alleviate the consistency bottleneck.
083              вҖў Multi-task fine-tuning is not promising to benefit the cross-lingual consistency as it poten-
084                tially refines specific attention heads for in-context information instead of knowledge.
085
086   Our contribution is to offer an understanding of multilingual language modelsвҖҷ limitations under
087   cross-lingual settings and highlight potential research directions to address such issues.
088
089
      2       M ETHODOLOGY
090
091
      2.1       TASK D EFINITION
092
093   We focus on a code-mixed context-independent cloze task. This setting forces the multilingual
094   model to rely on its internal knowledge base and recall the common knowledge shared by coreferen-
095   tial entities across languages because of cross-lingual generalization 1 . In the following introduction,
096   we will define the evaluation task mathematically. Let I = {S l1 , В· В· В· , O, В· В· В· } вҲҲ l12 be a statement,
097   where l1 stands for matrix language (the predominant language), S l1 = {s1 , В· В· В· , sk } вҲҲ l1 are sub-
098   ject sub-tokens, and O = {o1 , o2 , В· В· В· , oj } вҲҲ l1 denote object sub-tokens. This statement is used
099   to create a masked input Imono = {S l1 , В· В· В· , M, В· В· В· }, where M = {mask1 , В· В· В· , maskj } are the
100
      mask (or the sentinel token M =< extra id 0 >) used to substitute O in I. We define n-gram
      prediction for the mask O, denoted as Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ), as the top-k n-gram candidates with
101
      token lengths ranging from 1 to j obtained from beam search decoding over the modelвҖҷs vocabu-
102
      lary V . Considering the trade-off between the computational time and the holistic of the language
103   modelвҖҷs prediction, we set the top-k threshold and beam search width to 5. Similarly, we can create
104   a code-mixed coreferential statement Icm by replacing S l1 with a coreferential subject S l2 in the
105   embedded language l2 (the subsidiary language) in order to obtain Cand(OвҲҲV |Icm ). Therefore,
106
          1
107           See limitation in В§6.
          2
              The surface structure is not restricted. We use the common subjectвҖ“object structure as an example.


                                                               2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      Icm and Imono are coreferential and expected to recall the same knowledge. Finally, we define
109   cross-lingual knowledge consistency as 0 вүӨ fmetric (Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ), Cand(OвҲҲV |Icm )) вүӨ 1,
110   where fmetric is a consistency metric defined in the next subsection. If fmetric = 1, it implies that
111   multilingual language models recall factual knowledge for the coreferential statements Imono and
112   Icm in an identical manner. The coreferential statements disagree if fmetric = 0. Note that we do
113   not consider whether the prediction is correct. Instead, fmetric evaluates the parity and consistency
114   across the languages that the model is expected to output similar candidates for Imono and Icm .
115   From a probability view, we can define our task as measuring the difference between two dis-
116   tributions, Cand(OвҲҲV |Icm ) = P (OвҲҲV |KОё )P (KОё |S l2 , I\(S l1 вҲ©O) ) and Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ) =
117   P (OвҲҲV |KОёвҲ— )P (KОёвҲ— |S l1 , I\(S l1 вҲ©O) ), where KОё is the knowledge recalled from the model given
118   the preceding context, and I\(S l1 вҲ©O) stands for I without both the subject and the object. Then,
119   cross-lingual knowledge consistency between KОёвҲ— and KОё reflects on the measured difference. The
120   high-level idea of this evaluation task is illustrated in Figure 1 where en entry вҖқParis is the capi-
121   tal of вҖқ is evaluated with its possible code-mixed statements (ar entry & ta entry). In this ex-
122   ample, S l1 , I\(S l1 вҲ©O) , and S l2 are вҖқParisвҖқ, вҖқis the capital ofвҖқ, and the ar or ta entry for вҖқParisвҖқ,
123   respectively. If coreferential subject entries are trained to generalize across languages, we could
124   observe the cross-lingual consistency. In addition, we are aware of a baseline from this probabil-
125
      ity view. Specifically, we define the baseline as the difference between Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ) and
      Cand(OвҲҲV |I\(S l1 вҲ©O) ) = P (OвҲҲV |KОёОұ )P (KОёОұ |I\(S l1 вҲ©O) ), measuring agnostic consistency without
126
127   the coreferential subjects S l1 and S l2 in cross-lingual settings. In implementation, we mask the both
128
      subject and object entities to create the вҖқcode-mixedвҖқ counterpart as the baseline. Readers can refer
      to Appendix В§A.1 for our implementation.
129
130
      2.2   M ETRIC F UNCTION AND I NTERPRETABILITY A PPROACH
131
132   Readers can refer to Appendix В§A.2 for more details, e.g., equations.
133
134   Consistency Metrics. For fmetric , Top@1 Accuracy and RankC (i.e., weighted Preci-
135   sion@5) (Qi et al., 2023) are used to evaluate the cross-lingual knowledge consistency between
136   Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ) and Cand(OвҲҲV |Icm ). Since we observe similar experimental results on Top@1
137   and RankC, Top@1 results are moved to Appendix В§A.3.
138
139   Consistency Evolution. We analyze the вҖңevolutionвҖқ of consistency scores as the layer goes deeper
140   to trace the consistency bottleneck and understand the modelsвҖҷ behavior. Since the encoder part is
141   crucial for both encoder and encoder-decoder language models to understand the input, we apply
142   LogitLens (nostalgebraist, 2019) (for the xlm-r family) and DecoderLens (Langedijk et al., 2023)
143
      (for the mT0 family) to each encoder layer to obtain the layer-wise distribution OвҲҲV .
144
      SubjectвҖ“Object Attention. Inspired by the attention weight analysis method (Clark et al., 2019),
145
      we calculate the sum of all subject tokensвҖҷ attention scores across all masked tokens and average
146
      those scores over all possible Imono and Icm statements. Then we collect the difference between
147   average attention scores of Icm and those obtained from Imono . Note that masked tokens are used to
148   prompt the corresponding object tokens of the masked statements, as defined in our task definition.
149
150   IG2 Score We do minor modification on IG2 (Liu et al., 2024) to measure the impact of each
151   feed-forward neuron on the logits of the mask tokens where the higher the value is, the more critical
152   the neuron is to predict the ground truth object on the mask tokens.
153
154   2.3   DATASET AND MODEL
155
156   Dataset. We use mLAMA dataset (Kassner et al., 2021) that provides parallel triples (object, pred-
157
      icate, subject) in 53 languages written in cloze task format (e.g., вҖңParis is the capital of [MASK].вҖқ)
      to query knowledge in zero-shot settings. In our experiments, l1 is set to English. Meanwhile, we
158
      set l2 to all other 52 languages to report an overall result and rendered deep analysis for 2 similar l2
159
      languages (De, Id) and 2 dissimilar l2 languages (Ar, Ta)3 . Moreover, for the overall result, we also
160
          3
161         While Id does not belong to the same language family as En, it has many similarities with En (Krause,
      n.d.). Ar and Ta are not considered as Indo-European languages and also do not use latin scripts.


                                                          3
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


162
      categorize these l2 languages into two separate categories for each of the three factors (geographics,
163   writing scripts, and language family) using ISO-639 language code information from вҖқlocalizelyвҖқ4 .
164
165   Models. We examine distinct language model families: xlm-r (0.3B to 10B) (Conneau et al., 2020)
166   and mT0 (0.6B to 3.7B) (Muennighoff et al., 2023). Decoder-based language models are excluded
167   in our study to limit inherent hallucination problems affecting the analysis (Xu et al., 2024; Ji et al.,
168   2023; Fu et al., 2023). In our experiments, we obtain similar findings from both families. Therefore,
169   we only show mT0 results in the main text and move the rest to the Appendix В§A.3.

181
182   Figure 2: Overall cross-lingual consistency in mt0 (red: mt0-large, blue: mt0-base) grouped by 3
183   factors (left: geographics, mid: language family, right: writing scripts). Note: The dashed line here
184   is the average corresponding consistency scores of mt0-base across languages( cf. В§A.3.2. )
185
186   From Figure 2, across all the factors, l2 that are dissimilar with l1, tend to have lower consistency
187   than those are similar to l1. The difference in writing scripts plays the most important role among
188   the other two factors. Thus, the number of shared tokens between two languages could affect the
189   cross-lingual consistency, and that is orthogonal to the common view of cross-lingual transfer that
190   shared tokens are not necessary (K et al., 2020; Artetxe et al., 2020). Another intriguing finding
191   is that geographic factor also affects consistency and this could be attributed to common culture
192   and vocabulary (Zhao et al., 2024a). On the other hand, we suppose that other linguistic factors
193
      contributing to the cross-lingual performance (de Vries et al., 2022; Kann et al., 2017; Chronopoulou
      et al., 2023) such as the similarity in linguistic features (Chronopoulou et al., 2023), or borrowing
194
      (Tsvetkov & Dyer, 2016), could affect cross-lingual knowledge consistency as well. However, for
195
      this study, it is hard to quantify such factors and leave such analyses for future work. Furthermore,
196   we also observe similar results on other models, and this aligns with empirical studies in the literature
197   (Qi et al., 2023). Note that language families and writing scripts have an impact on vocabulary, and
198   we will discuss this vocabulary problem in a later section.
199
      To better understand the cross-lingual consistency bottleneck, we examine the layer-wise consis-
200
      tency patterns across different model sizes, as presented in Figure 3. The noticeable difference lies
201
      in the initial consistency, whereby dissimilar language pairs have low consistency scores. The con-
202   sistency gap between dissimilar and similar languages starts to close at some specific layer while
203   widening again later. This observation provides evidence for empirical studies that scaling benefits
204   the downstream task performance (Conneau et al., 2020), e.g., XNLI, but not be substantially helpful

      4                   I MPROVING C ONSISTENCY

272
      4.1                     C AN B IAS C ALIBRATE C ONSISTENCY ?
273
274   From previous findings, we think of one question: can we add biases from Imono to attention layers
275   and feed-forward layers for consistency calibration? Thus, based on two different patterns dis-
276   covered from our experiments and having that both are moderately correlated with the consistency
277   score, we do three different causal interventions to align the output of Icm closer to the output of
278   Imono . This experiment measures whether each pattern has a causal relationship with cross-lingual
279   consistency. The experimental setup can be seen in Appendix В§A.4.1. We consider: Attention
280   suppression: suppressing Icm вҖҷs attention scores to make them closer to Imono вҖҷs, Feed-forward
281   neuron activation patching (Vig et al., 2020; Geiger et al., 2021): patching Imono вҖҷs activations
282
      of all tokens to Icm in selected feed-forward neurons based on IG2 , and Hybrid: using the above
      methods simultaneously.
283

                                                                         Figure 6: Intervention scores for EnвҖ“Ta. ( cf. В§A.4.1).
294
295   Based on Figure 6, there is a causal relationship between the two studied factors to some certain
296   extent. For mT0, intervention approaches can increase the consistency scores in the middle-later
297   layers only. While for xlm-r, none of the interventions manages to improve the consistency for the
298   base model despite there is a rising trend for the hybrid intervention and feed-forward activation
299   patching. However, when we observe the larger model, all intervention methods increase the con-
300   sistency score on the last layers. Overall, our monolingual bias incorporation offers quite limited
301   improvement subject to the architecture and model size thus a better monolingual bias should be
302   considered. These findings are consistent with those of other languages.
303
304   4.2                     T HE E FFECT OF VOCABULARY E XPANSION TO T HE C ROSS - LINGUAL C ONSISTENCY
305
306   We hypothesize that the size of vocabulary plays a crucial role in improving consistency as this
307   enables a language model to align the semantics better due to lesser ambiguity5 . To test this hy-
308
      pothesis, we consider two similar language models, xlm-r-base and xlm-v-base (Liang et al., 2023),
      where xlm-v-base has larger vocabularies (901,629 tokens) than xlm-r-base (250,002 tokens).
309
310                  5
          e.g., if the tokenizer of a language model tokenizes the word вҖқTokyoвҖқ to [вҖқTo,вҖқ вҖқKyoвҖқ], the token вҖқToвҖқ is
311   polysemous making thus the alignment of this word would be one-to-many, on the other hand, if a tokenizer
312   keeps the word as it is, the tokenized form of the word is monosemous making it less ambiguous.

403   baseline as we would expect. The aligned model outperforms the baseline starting from the middle
404   layers in Figure 10. Multiple pre-training objectives that could approximately disentangle different
405   features can help preserve the modelвҖҷs knowledge of different languages. We could also confirm
406   this finding by observing the overall cross-lingual consistency result in Figure 12, as consistency
407   scores jump over the baseline modelвҖҷs cross-lingual consistency. When we look closer at the object-
408   subject attention scores of the aligned model in Figure 11, there is a slight shift of subjectвҖ“object
409   relation features extraction from earlier layers (in the baseline model) into middle-last layers (in the
410
      aligned model). In line with the vocabulary expansion, the responsibility shift on feature extraction
      from the earlier layer into later layers might justify the effectiveness of both approaches on dissim-
411
      ilar languages. Interestingly, the attention shift is not as strong as the one caused by vocabulary
412
      expansion, which is quite counterintuitive as cross-lingual supervision outperforms vocabulary ex-
413   pansion in improving cross-lingual consistency. Hence, we leave this interesting finding analysis
414   for future work. In addition, word alignments improve consistency for transliterations or similar
415   orthographical forms, contributing to modelвҖҷs robustness against orthographic variations and non-
416   standard spellings, but vocabulary expansion can not offer such gains.(cf. В§A.4.4)

      can see one similar layer shift pattern with experiments on the vocabulary expansion and the cross-
      lingual supervision where the attention weights are suppressed on earlier layers. However, unlike

      across mt0 and mt5. Instead of shifting layers for the attention, Figure 15: Effects of multi-
      which is observed in other experiments, multi-task fine-tuning task fine-tuning on the layer-
478
      causes a head shift in Figure 16. We suspect that the model ad- wise consistency. (cf. 4.3)
479
      justs some neurons at each layer to maintain knowledge but such
480   adjustment has limited contributions to cross-lingual consistency. While this finding is distinguish-
481   able from most of other methods, it is consistent with Figure 4, where we found scaling is not
482   promising to improve cross-lingual consistency. Specifically, both of them encourage some neurons
483   to preserve the cross-lingual knowledge consistency, showing limited effectiveness. This finding
484   aligns with Ortu et al. (2024); Jin et al. (2024) who reported that LLM has attention heads with
485   contrasting roles in which some of them consider retrieving internal knowledge of language models
      and other heads prefer to get the in-context information.


                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      5   R ELATED W ORK
487
488
489
      Petroni et al. (2019) found the availability of relational knowledge within the pre-trained language
      model by evaluating the language models on the cloze task dataset they proposed, namely LAMA.
490
      Kassner et al. (2021) introduced the multilingual version of it, mLAMA, and discovered that the
491
      languageвҖҷs relational knowledge capability varies in different languages and other works also found
492   similar findings (Schott et al., 2023; Zhao et al., 2024a). Nevertheless, Zhao et al. (2024a) showed
493   that multilingual language models exhibited limited cross-lingual knowledge recall capability on
494   low-resource languages. Following this line, Fierro & SГёgaard (2022); Qi et al. (2023) studied the
495   final predictions in different languages and reported inconsistencies across languages. Moreover,
496   Jin et al. (2024) proposed a method to mitigate such conflicting mechanisms by nullifying heads
497   having significant impact in either of both roles. We take a different angle from those works where
498   we evaluate the cross-lingual knowledge consistency against references in different languages by
499   creating coreferential statements in cross-lingual settings.
500   Bhattacharya & Bojar (2023); Kojima et al. (2024); Zhao et al. (2024b) discovered the language-
501   sensitive neurons of decoder in the early and last layers while a considerable portion of language-
502   agnostic ones in the middle layers encode universal concepts and utilize the latent language (in
503   this case English) (Wendler et al., 2024; Dumas et al., 2024). Tan et al. (2024) observed encoder-
504   decoder language models that neurons tend to be more language-agnostic in the later layers of the
505   encoder part while language-specific in the later layers of the decoder part. Zhao et al. (2024b);
506
      Wang et al. (2024b); Zhang et al. (2024) further showed the cross-lingual downstream performance
      is potentially proportional to the amount of language-agnostic neurons. Ferrando & Costa-jussaМҖ
507
      (2024) discovered a shared circuit or subnetwork that is responsible for subject-verb agreement task
508
      for English & Spanish and Stanczak et al. (2022); Wang et al. (2024a) found that morpho-syntax
509   attributes have noticeable neuron overlapping degree over notable amount of language pairs. We
510   push this line further to trace consistent information and knowledge throughout the layers in cross-
511   lingual settings, attempting to understand and interpret how commonly used strategies to improve
512   multilingual models for downstream tasks could impact the cross-lingual knowledge consistency.
513
514
515   6   C ONCLUSION
516
517   Do multilingual language models demonstrate cross-lingual consistency? Is it worthwhile to op-
518   timise for cross-lingual knowledge consistency? We find the answer to both of these questions is
519   вҖҳyesвҖҷ, but with the caveat that performance is tied to language characteristics. In our work, we code
520   mix source monolingual sentences containing a coreferential named entity to control and analyze
521   cross-lingual knowledge consistency.
522   Our analysis reveals that knowledge consistency is heavily dependent on language-specific informa-
523   tion such as geography, language family, and writing script. Our layer-by-layer analysis of multi-
524   lingual models discovers a consistency bottleneck in the middle layers of models. This bottleneck
525   can be alleviated by expanding the vocabulary, injecting cross-lingual supervision and in training, or
526   including code-swithcing corpus. Our work highlights promising directions in post-calibration, vo-
527   cabulary formation, pretraining with cross-lingual objectives, and code-switching training to achieve
528   knowledge consistency across languages, which will better preserve parity of language model per-
529   formance. As our experiment discovers that pretraining objective and code-switching training cause
530
      most significant positive impact on the cross-lingual consistency, we encourage researchers to em-
      phasize more on representation learning approaches to make the language models more consistent
531
      across different languages.

756
      L IMITATIONS
757
758
      We only cover the transformer encoder and encoder-language models for this work. Another promis-
759   ing avenue for this work is evaluating cross-lingual knowledge consistency on other language mod-
760   els. Moreover, we only analyze each crucial component independently due to the time constraint
761   and left scrutinizing the interaction between each component for future work. In the future, we may
762   expand this work by analyzing how the interaction among these components could affect the cross-
763   lingual consistency of multilingual models. Another thing is that our causal intervention method
764   needs to be done manually, and we suspect that this method could produce a side effect on the
765   model because the representations encoded by language models are more likely to be polysemous.
766   Additionally, we only evaluate the language models in context-independent settings. Thus, in the
767
      future, we plan to evaluate the consistency of the modelsвҖҷ knowledge and observe whether language
      models utilize their parametric knowledge more or emphasize the knowledge from the given context
768
      under the cross-lingual setting. Another thing to consider is that we only evaluate our solution using
769
      one particular model due to the time constraint. Also, we do not explore various pre-training ob-
770   jectives and evaluate solutions to the encoder-decoder model; hence, we leave such things as future
771   work. One interesting thing to explore in this aspect is to see whether adversarial training could
772   help to enhance cross-lingual consistency. Another thing that we want to consider is that we use an
773   assumption that one reference is represented as a single English object entity to make the evaluation
774   tractable; hence, we do not take into account the real-world setting where one reference can be inter-
775   preted in different ways on multiple languages (e.g., вҖқChinaвҖқ is written as вҖқZhongGuoвҖқ in Chinese
776   rather than вҖқChinaвҖқ). Lastly, our research scope assumes that the knowledge we want to evaluate is
777   factual and not dependent on subjective aspects (e.g., cultural context). With that assumption, we
778
      assume that references here generally have one-to-one mapping to representation in one language
      where the representation here is considered common knowledge.
779
780
781   E THICS S TATEMENT
782
783   This work aims to evaluate the consistency of the language model across different senses (partic-
784   ularly between a monolingual input and its code-mixed counterparts) and the impact of different
785   factors on that metric. Doing such a study could shed light on the limitations of language models
786   and think of the mitigations of such matters.
787
788   R EPRODUCIBILITY S TATEMENTS
789
      We used open-source pretrained models and also dataset for all of the reported experi-
790
      ments thus no undisclosed assets utilized in our work.  Additionally, we also provide
791   necessary experimentsвҖҷ output and codes on https://anonymous.4open.science/r/
792   knowledgeConsistencyAndConflict-4827.

      A.2   M ETRIC F UNCTION AND I NTERPRETABILITY A PPROACH
865
866   RankC RankC (Qi et al., 2023) is used to evaluate the cross-lingual knowledge consistency. Given
867   a set of statements S where each of the statement having each own Imono and Icm , the number
868   of candidates Cand(OвҲҲV |Imono ) of i-th statement Ni , monoj stands for the j-th candidate of

1468                            Table 3: Causal Intervention Hyperparameters Setup
1469
1470
1471   A.4     I MPROVING C ONSISTENCY
1472
1473   A.4.1    A DDING M ONOLINGUAL B IAS
1474
       This experiment aims to measure whether each pattern has a causal relationship with cross-lingual
1475   consistency.
1476
1477           вҖў Attention score suppression: Using the definition from A.2 and define a suppression con-
1478             stant Оұ, Оұ вҲҲ [0, 1), the patched attention weight of every object-subject relation will be
1479             AвҲ—a,b = ОұAa,b .
1480           вҖў Feed-forward neuron activation patching (Vig et al., 2020; Geiger et al., 2021): consider
1481               (l,p)
                 ai      as the activation of i-th token on Imono produced by p-th neuron in l-th encoder
1482             layerвҖҷs feed-forward network, then patched activation value for the i-th token on Icm is
1483               (l,p)     (l,p)
                 aМ„i     = ai , in which we apply this for every mask token.
1484
1485           вҖў Hybrid: We apply attention weight suppression and feed-forward neuron activations patch-
1486
                 ing simultaneously.
1487
       For the hyperparameters used in the causal intervention experiment, we set the Оұ value that is not
1488   too big so that did not significantly diminish the attention weight yet making the attention weight
1489   distribution for Icm closer to that weight distribution for Imono . While for FFN-layers, we intervene
1490   4 different encoder layers for base models and 5 different encoder layers that have language-sensitive
1491   neurons based on IG2 (i.e. layer which has noticeable IG2 distribution difference between Imono
1492   and Ic m). Readers can refer to table 3 to see the hyperparameters used in this experiment.

1944
       A.4.4                 C ASE S TUDY FOR T RANSLITERATION
1945
1946   Instead of using translations, we transliterate bn7 and ar8 to understand the impact of writing sys-
1947   tems, particularly transliterations. As presented in Figure 38, word alignments (or the similar effect
1948   from CS training) contribute to the modelвҖҷs cross-lingual consistency against writing systems be-
1949   cause xlm-align and xlm-r-cs show similar performance in both original and transliteration settings.
1950
       Meanwhile, we can observe that xlm-align and xlm-r-cs significantly improve the overall perfor-
       mance for non-Lattin scripts in В§A.4.3. This is reasonable as word alignments or CS training help
1951
       the model link original words with their translations or transliterations, depending on the training
1952
       corpus, thereby enhancing cross-lingual consistency. We suspect that these word alignments might
1953   also improve robustness for handling non-standard spellings and orthographic variations. However,
1954   xlm-v-base and xlm-r-base without word alignment benefit from transliterations, which means that
1955   xlm-v-base and xlm-r-base do not sufficiently align original words with their transliterations to main
1956   cross-lingual consistency. It is also confirmed by the overall performance of vocabulary expansions
1957   in В§A.4.2, where vocabulary expansions can not offer significant gains for cross-lingual consistency.
1958   Overall, the evaluation task does not inadequately boost consistency for languages using Latin script
1959   because word alignments resulting in cross-lingual consistency are the main factor.
1960
